font-weight: normal;"><a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">Republicanism</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;padding:0 0 1.4em;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Consul_et_lictores.png"><img alt="Consul et lictores.png" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Consul_et_lictores.png/100px-Consul_et_lictores.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Consul_et_lictores.png/150px-Consul_et_lictores.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Consul_et_lictores.png/200px-Consul_et_lictores.png 2x" width="100"/></a></td></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:transparent; style="font-size:105%;background:transparent;text-align:left;background:transparent; border-top: 1px solid #aaa; text-align: center; padding-left: 0.4em; padding-right: 2.4em;">Related topics</div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Communitarianism" title="Communitarianism">Communitarianism</a></li>\n<li><a title="Monarchism">Monarchism</a></li></ul></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;text-align: center;">\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r872444996">.mw-parser-output .nobold{font-weight:normal}</style><span class="nobold"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Politics" title="Portal:Politics">Politics portal</a></span></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Republicanism_sidebar" title="Template:Republicanism sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Republicanism_sidebar" title="Template talk:Republicanism sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Republicanism_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Thomas Jefferson</b> (April 13, [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> April 2]&#160;1743&#160;&#8211; July 4, 1826) was a statesman, diplomat, architect, and <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Father</a> who served as the third <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">president of the United States</a> from 1801 to 1809. Previously, he had been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1796" title="United States presidential election, 1796">elected</a> the second <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vice_president_of_the_United_States" title="Vice president of the United States">vice president of the United States</a>, serving under <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> from 1797 to 1801. The principal author of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>, Jefferson was a proponent of democracy, <a href="/wiki/Republicanism" title="Republicanism">republicanism</a>, and individual rights motivating <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Colonies" title="Thirteen Colonies">American colonists</a> to break from the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a> and form a new nation; he produced formative documents and decisions at both the state and national level.\n</p><p>Jefferson was mainly of English ancestry, born and educated in <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">colonial Virginia</a>. He graduated from the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a> and briefly practiced law, with the largest number of his cases concerning land ownership claims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a>, he represented Virginia in the <a href="/wiki/Continental_Congress" title="Continental Congress">Continental Congress</a> that adopted the Declaration, drafted the law for religious freedom as a Virginia legislator, and served as a wartime governor (1779&#8211;1781). He became the United States Minister to France in May 1785, and subsequently the nation\'s first <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">secretary of state</a> in 1790&#8211;1793 under <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">President George Washington</a>. Jefferson and <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a> organized the <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> to oppose the <a href="/wiki/Federalist_Party" title="Federalist Party">Federalist Party</a> during the formation of the <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a>. With Madison, he anonymously wrote the controversial <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_and_Virginia_Resolutions" title="Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions">Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions</a> in 1798&#8211;1799, which sought to strengthen <a href="/wiki/States%27_rights" title="States\' rights">states\' rights</a> by nullifying the federal <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>.\n</p><p>As President, Jefferson pursued the nation\'s shipping and trade interests against <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">Barbary pirates</a> and aggressive British trade policies. He also organized the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a>, almost doubling the country\'s territory. As a result of peace negotiations with France, his administration reduced military forces. He was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1804" title="United States presidential election, 1804">reelected in 1804</a>. Jefferson\'s second term was beset with difficulties at home, including the trial of former Vice President <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>. American foreign trade was diminished when Jefferson implemented the <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act_of_1807" title="Embargo Act of 1807">Embargo Act of 1807</a>, responding to British threats to U.S. shipping. In 1803, Jefferson began a controversial process of Indian tribe removal to the newly organized <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Territory" title="Louisiana Territory">Louisiana Territory</a>, and he signed the <a href="/wiki/Act_Prohibiting_Importation_of_Slaves" title="Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves">Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves</a> in 1807.\n</p><p>Jefferson, while primarily a <a href="/wiki/Plantations_in_the_American_South#Plantation_owner" title="Plantations in the American South">planter</a>, lawyer and politician, mastered many disciplines, which ranged from surveying and mathematics to <a href="/wiki/Horticulture" title="Horticulture">horticulture</a> and mechanics. He was an architect in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">classical tradition</a>. Jefferson\'s keen interest in religion and philosophy led to his presidency of the <a href="/wiki/American_Philosophical_Society" title="American Philosophical Society">American Philosophical Society</a>; he shunned organized religion but was influenced by both Christianity and <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>. A <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philologist</a>, Jefferson knew several languages. He was a prolific letter writer and corresponded with many prominent people. His only full-length book is <i><a href="/wiki/Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia" title="Notes on the State of Virginia">Notes on the State of Virginia</a></i> (1785), considered perhaps the most important American book published before 1800.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> After retiring from public office, Jefferson founded the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a>.\n</p><p>Although regarded as a leading spokesman for democracy and republicanism in the era of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>, Jefferson\'s historical legacy is mixed. Some modern scholarship has been critical of Jefferson\'s private life, pointing out the contradiction between his ownership of the large numbers of slaves that worked his plantations and his famous declaration that "all men are created equal." Another point of controversy stems from the evidence that after his wife <a href="/wiki/Martha_Jefferson" title="Martha Jefferson">Martha</a> died in 1782, Jefferson fathered children with Martha\'s half-sister, <a href="/wiki/Sally_Hemings" title="Sally Hemings">Sally Hemings</a>, who was his slave. Despite this, presidential scholars and historians generally praise his public achievements, including his advocacy of religious freedom and tolerance in Virginia. Jefferson continues to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="Historical rankings of Presidents of the United States">rank highly</a> among U.S. presidents.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" href="#Member_of_Congress"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Member of Congress</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Minister_to_France"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Minister to France</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Secretary_of_State"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span of 1800</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Presidency_(1801&#8211;1809)"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Presidency (1801&#8211;1809)</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#First_Barbary_War"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">First Barbary War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Louisiana_Purchase"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Louisiana Purchase</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Burr_conspiracy_and_trial"><span class="tocnumber">3.6</span> <span class="toctext">Burr conspiracy and trial</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Chesapeake&#8211;Leopard_affair_and_Embargo_Act"><span class="tocnumber">3.7</span> <span class="toctext"><i>Chesapeake</i>&#8211;<i>Leopard</i> affair and Embargo Act</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Post-presidency_(1809&#8211;1826)"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Post-presidency (1809&#8211;1826)</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#University_of_Virginia"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">University of Virginia</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Reconciliation_with_Adams"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Reconciliation with Adams</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Autobiography"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Autobiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-26"><a href="#Lafayette\'s_visit"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Lafayette\'s visit</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Final_days,_death,_and_burial"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Final days, death, and burial</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Political,_social_and_religious_views"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Political, social and religious views</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Society_and_government"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Society and government</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Democracy"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Democracy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Banks"><span class="tocnumber">5.4</span> <span class="toctext">Banks</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-33"><a href="#Slavery"><span class="tocnumber">5.5</span> <span class="toctext">Slavery</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-34"><a href="#Historical_assessment"><span class="tocnumber">5.5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Historical assessment</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-35"><a href="#Jefferson&#8211;Hemings_controversy"><span class="tocnumber">5.5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Jefferson&#8211;Hemings controversy</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Interests_and_activities"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Interests and activities</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#American_Philosophical_Society"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">American Philosophical Society</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-38"><a href="#Linguistics"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">Linguistics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Inventions"><span class="tocnumber">6.3</span> <span class="toctext">Inventions</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-40"><a href="#Historical_reputation"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Historical reputation</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Memorials_and_honors"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Memorials and honors</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42"><a href="#Writings"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Writings</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Ancestry"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Ancestry</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-44"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-45"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_career">Early life and career</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_and_career_of_Thomas_Jefferson" title="Early life and career of Thomas Jefferson">Early life and career of Thomas Jefferson</a></div>\n<p>Thomas Jefferson was born on April 13, 1743 (April 2, 1743, <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">Old Style</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>), at the family home in <a href="/wiki/Shadwell,_Virginia" title="Shadwell, Virginia">Shadwell</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">Colony of Virginia</a>, the third of ten children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> He was of English, and possibly Welsh, descent and was born a British subject.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malone5_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malone5-4">[4]</a></sup> His father <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jefferson" title="Peter Jefferson">Peter Jefferson</a> was a planter and surveyor who died when Jefferson was fourteen; his mother was <a href="/wiki/Jane_Randolph_Jefferson" title="Jane Randolph Jefferson">Jane Randolph</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[a]</a></sup> Peter Jefferson moved his family to <a href="/wiki/Tuckahoe_(plantation)" title="Tuckahoe (plantation)">Tuckahoe Plantation</a> in 1745 upon the death of William Randolph, the plantation\'s owner and Jefferson\'s friend, who in his will had named him guardian of his children. The Jeffersons returned to Shadwell in 1752, where Peter died in 1757; his estate was divided between his sons Thomas and <a href="/wiki/Randolph_Jefferson" title="Randolph Jefferson">Randolph</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> Thomas inherited approximately 5,000 acres (2,000&#160;ha; 7.8&#160;sq&#160;mi) of land, including <a href="/wiki/Monticello" title="Monticello">Monticello</a>. He assumed full authority over his property at age 21.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Malone437_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Malone437-8">[7]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Education.2C_early_family_life"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Education,_early_family_life">Education, early family life</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building,_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg,_Virginia,_USA_(2008-04-23).jpg"><img alt="A university building" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg/220px-Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg/330px-Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg/440px-Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building%2C_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg%2C_Virginia%2C_USA_%282008-04-23%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rear_view_of_the_Wren_Building,_College_of_William_%26_Mary_in_Williamsburg,_Virginia,_USA_(2008-04-23).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Wren Building (rear), College of William &amp; Mary where Jefferson studied</div></div></div>\n<p>Jefferson began his childhood education beside the <a href="/wiki/Randolph_family_of_Virginia" title="Randolph family of Virginia">Randolph</a> children with tutors at <a href="/wiki/Tuckahoe,_Virginia" title="Tuckahoe, Virginia">Tuckahoe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> Thomas\' father, Peter, was self-taught, and regretting not having a formal education, he entered Thomas into an English school early, at age five. In 1752, at age nine, he began attending a local school run by a Scottish Presbyterian minister and also began studying the natural world, for which he grew to love. At this time he began studying Latin, Greek, and French, while also learning to ride horses. Thomas also read books from his father\'s modest library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowers12_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-bowers12-10">[9]</a></sup> He was taught from 1758 to 1760 by Reverend <a href="/wiki/James_Maury" title="James Maury">James Maury</a> near <a href="/wiki/Gordonsville,_Virginia" title="Gordonsville, Virginia">Gordonsville, Virginia</a>, where he studied history, science, and the classics while boarding with Maury\'s family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bowers12_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-bowers12-10">[9]</a></sup> During this period Jefferson came to know and befriended various American Indians, including the famous Cherokee chief, <i>Ontassete</i>, who often stopped at Shadwell to visit, on their way to Williamsburg to trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup> During the two years Jefferson was with the Maury family, he traveled to Williamsburg and was a guest of <a href="/wiki/John_Dandridge" title="John Dandridge">Colonel Dandridge</a>, father of <a href="/wiki/Martha_Washington" title="Martha Washington">Martha Washington</a>. In Williamsburg the young Jefferson met and came to admire <a href="/wiki/Patrick_Henry" title="Patrick Henry">Patrick Henry</a>, who was eight years his senior, sharing a common interest of violin playing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson entered the <a href="/wiki/College_of_William_%26_Mary" title="College of William &amp; Mary">College of William &amp; Mary</a> in <a href="/wiki/Williamsburg,_Virginia" title="Williamsburg, Virginia">Williamsburg, Virginia</a>, at age 16 and studied mathematics, metaphysics, and philosophy under Professor <a href="/wiki/William_Small" title="William Small">William Small</a>. Small introduced Jefferson to <a href="/wiki/George_Wythe" title="George Wythe">George Wythe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francis_Fauquier" title="Francis Fauquier">Francis Fauquier</a> along with <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">British Empiricists</a> including <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>. Small, Wythe and Fauquier recognized Jefferson as a man of exceptional ability and included him in their inner circle where he became a regular member of their Friday dinner parties where politics and philosophy were discussed. Jefferson later wrote that he "heard more common good sense, more rational &amp; philosophical conversations than in all the rest of my life".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> During his first year at the college he was given more to parties, dancing and was not very frugal with his expenditures; during his second year, regretting that he had squandered away much time and money, he applied himself to fifteen hours of study a day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Jefferson improved his French and Greek and his skill at the violin. He graduated two years after starting in 1762. He <a href="/wiki/Reading_law" title="Reading law">read the law</a> under Professor Wythe\'s tutelage to obtain his law license, while working as a <a href="/wiki/Law_clerk" title="Law clerk">law clerk</a> in his office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> He also read a wide variety of English classics and political works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup> Jefferson was well read in a broad variety of subjects, which along with law and philosophy, included history, natural law, natural religion, ethics, and several areas in science, including agriculture. Overall, he drew very deeply on the philosophers. During the years of study under the watchful eye of Wythe, Jefferson authored a survey of his extensive readings in his <i>Commonplace Book</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> So impressed with Jefferson, Wythe would later bequeath his entire library to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>1765 was an eventful year in Jefferson\'s family. In July, his sister Martha married his close friend and college companion <a href="/wiki/Dabney_Carr" title="Dabney Carr">Dabney Carr</a>, which greatly pleased Jefferson. In October, he mourned his sister Jane\'s unexpected death at age 25 and wrote a farewell epitaph in Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>\nJefferson treasured his books. In 1770, his Shadwell home was destroyed by fire, including a library of 200 volumes inherited from his father and those left to him by George Wythe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> Nevertheless, he had replenished his library with 1,250 titles by 1773, and his collection grew to almost 6,500 volumes in 1814.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> The British burned the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a> that year; he then sold more than 6,000 books to the Library for $23,950. He had intended to pay off some of his large debt, but he resumed collecting for his personal library, writing to <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a>, "I cannot live without books."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-library_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-library-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lawyer_and_House_of_Burgesses">Lawyer and House of Burgesses</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg"><img alt="Chamber of House of Burgesses" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="763" data-file-width="971" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg/220px-House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg/330px-House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg/440px-House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:House_of_Burgesses_in_the_Capitol_Williamsburg_James_City_County_Virginia_by_Frances_Benjamin_Johnston.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>House of Burgesses in Williamsburg, Virginia, where Jefferson served 1769&#8211;1775</div></div></div>\n<p>Jefferson was admitted to the Virginia bar in 1767 and then lived with his mother at Shadwell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> In addition to practicing law, Jefferson represented <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County,_Virginia" title="Albemarle County, Virginia">Albemarle County</a> as a delegate in the Virginia <a href="/wiki/House_of_Burgesses" title="House of Burgesses">House of Burgesses</a> from 1769 until 1775.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup> He pursued reforms to slavery. He introduced legislation in 1769 allowing masters to take control over the emancipation of slaves, taking discretion away from the royal governor and General Court. He persuaded his cousin <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bland" title="Richard Bland">Richard Bland</a> to spearhead the legislation\'s passage, but reaction was strongly negative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson took seven cases for freedom-seeking slaves<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup> and waived his fee for one client, who claimed that he should be freed before the statutory age of thirty-one required for emancipation in cases with inter-racial grandparents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon99_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon99-30">[29]</a></sup> He invoked the <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">Natural Law</a> to argue, "everyone comes into the world with a right to his own person and using it at his own will&#160;... This is what is called personal liberty, and is given him by the author of nature, because it is necessary for his own sustenance." The judge cut him off and ruled against his client. As a consolation, Jefferson gave his client some money, conceivably used to aid his escape shortly thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordon99_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon99-30">[29]</a></sup> He later incorporated this sentiment into the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup> He also took on 68 cases for the General Court of Virginia in 1767, in addition to three notable cases: <i>Howell v. Netherland</i> (1770), <i>Bolling v. Bolling</i> (1771), and <i>Blair v. Blair</i> (1772).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Konig_Time_Line_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Konig_Time_Line-32">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British Parliament passed the <a href="/wiki/Intolerable_Acts" title="Intolerable Acts">Intolerable Acts</a> in 1774, and Jefferson wrote a resolution calling for a "Day of Fasting and Prayer" in protest, as well as a boycott of all British goods. His resolution was later expanded into <i><a href="/wiki/A_Summary_View_of_the_Rights_of_British_America" title="A Summary View of the Rights of British America">A Summary View of the Rights of British America</a></i>, in which he argued that people have the right to <a href="/wiki/Self-governing_colony" title="Self-governing colony">govern themselves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Monticello.2C_marriage_and_family"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Monticello,_marriage_and_family">Monticello, marriage and family</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg"><img alt="Monticello plantation house" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg/220px-Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg/330px-Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg/440px-Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Monticello_2010-10-29.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jefferson\'s home Monticello</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1768, Jefferson began constructing his primary residence Monticello (Italian for "Little Mountain") on a hilltop overlooking his 5,000-acre (20&#160;km<sup>2</sup>; 7.8&#160;sq&#160;mi) plantation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[b]</a></sup> Construction was done mostly by local masons and carpenters, assisted by Jefferson\'s slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He moved into the South Pavilion in 1770. Turning Monticello into a neoclassical masterpiece in the <a href="/wiki/Palladian_architecture" title="Palladian architecture">Palladian</a> style was his perennial project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>\nOn January 1, 1772, Jefferson married his third cousin Martha Wayles Skelton, the 23-year-old widow of Bathurst Skelton, and she moved into the South Pavilion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tucker_p.47_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker_p.47-38">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> She was a frequent hostess for Jefferson and managed the large household. Biographer <a href="/wiki/Dumas_Malone" title="Dumas Malone">Dumas Malone</a> described the marriage as the happiest period of Jefferson\'s life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[38]</a></sup> Martha read widely, did fine needlework, and was a skilled pianist; Jefferson often accompanied her on the violin or cello.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> During their ten years of marriage, Martha bore six children: <a href="/wiki/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Martha Jefferson Randolph">Martha</a> "Patsy" (1772&#8211;1836); Jane (1774&#8211;1775); a son who lived for only a few weeks in 1777; <a href="/wiki/Mary_Jefferson_Eppes" title="Mary Jefferson Eppes">Mary Wayles</a> "Polly" (1778&#8211;1804); Lucy Elizabeth (1780&#8211;1781); and another Lucy Elizabeth (1782&#8211;1785).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Only Martha and Mary survived more than a few years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martha_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martha-42">[40]</a></sup> </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="620" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg/180px-Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg/270px-Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg/360px-Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Martha_Jefferson_Randolph_portrait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Martha Jefferson Randolph">Jefferson\'s daughter Martha</a></div></div></div><p> Martha\'s father John Wayles died in 1773, and the couple inherited 135 people of color who were legally enslaved, 11,000 acres (45&#160;km<sup>2</sup>; 17&#160;sq&#160;mi), and the estate\'s debts. The debts took Jefferson years to satisfy, contributing to his financial problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tucker_p.47_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tucker_p.47-38">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Martha later suffered from ill health, including diabetes, and frequent childbirth further weakened her. Her mother had died young, and Martha lived with two stepmothers as a girl. A few months after the birth of her last child, she died on September 6, 1782, at the age of 33 with Jefferson at her bedside. Shortly before her death, Martha made Jefferson promise never to marry again, telling him that she could not bear to have another mother raise her children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[41]</a></sup> Jefferson was grief-stricken by her death, relentlessly pacing back and forth, nearly to the point of exhaustion. He emerged after three weeks, taking long rambling rides on secluded roads with his daughter Martha, by her description "a solitary witness to many a violent burst of grief".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martha_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Martha-42">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halliday48_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halliday48-44">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After working as Secretary of State (1790&#8211;93), he returned to Monticello and initiated a remodeling based on the architectural concepts which he had acquired in Europe. The work continued throughout most of his presidency, being finished in 1809.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Political_career_1775.E2.80.931800"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_career_1775&#8211;1800">Political career 1775&#8211;1800</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Declaration_of_Independence">Declaration of Independence</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg"><img alt="Declaration of Independence" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4656" data-file-width="3923" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg/220px-United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg/330px-United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg/440px-United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:United_States_Declaration_of_Independence.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Declaration of Independence &#8211; 1823 facsimile of the engrossed copy</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a></div>\n<p>Jefferson was the primary author of the Declaration of Independence. The document\'s social and political ideals were proposed by Jefferson before the inauguration of Washington.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> At age 33, he was one of the youngest delegates to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> beginning in 1775 at the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, where a formal declaration of independence from Britain was overwhelmingly favored.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup> Jefferson chose his words for the Declaration in June 1775, shortly after the war had begun, where the idea of independence from Britain had long since become popular among the colonies. He was inspired by the Enlightenment ideals of the sanctity of the individual, as well as by the writings of Locke and Montesquieu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He sought out John Adams, an emerging leader of the Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup> They became close friends and Adams supported Jefferson\'s appointment to the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Five" title="Committee of Five">Committee of Five</a> formed to draft a declaration of independence in furtherance of the <a href="/wiki/Lee_Resolution" title="Lee Resolution">Lee Resolution</a> passed by the Congress, which declared the United Colonies independent. The committee initially thought that Adams should write the document, but Adams persuaded the committee to choose Jefferson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[c]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson consulted with other committee members over the next seventeen days, and drew on his own proposed draft of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Virginia" title="Constitution of Virginia">Virginia Constitution</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Mason" title="George Mason">George Mason</a>\'s draft of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Declaration_of_Rights" title="Virginia Declaration of Rights">Virginia Declaration of Rights</a>, and other sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> The other committee members made some changes, and a final draft was presented to the Congress on June 28, 1776.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham,_2012,_p._105_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham,_2012,_p._105-54">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The declaration was introduced on Friday, June 28, and congress began debate over its contents on Monday, July 1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham,_2012,_p._105_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham,_2012,_p._105-54">[51]</a></sup> resulting in the omission of a fourth of the text, including a passage critical of King George III and the slave trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis,_1996,_p._50_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis,_1996,_p._50-55">[52]</a></sup> Jefferson resented the changes, but he did not speak publicly about the revisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[d]</a></sup> On July 4, 1776, the Congress <a href="/wiki/Ratification#United_States" title="Ratification">ratified</a> the Declaration, and delegates signed it on August 2; in doing so, they were committing an act of treason against the Crown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[54]</a></sup> Jefferson\'s preamble is regarded as an enduring statement of human rights, and the phrase "<a href="/wiki/All_men_are_created_equal" title="All men are created equal">all men are created equal</a>" has been called "one of the best-known sentences in the English language" containing "the most potent and consequential words in American history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis,_1996,_p._50_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis,_1996,_p._50-55">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virginia_state_legislator_and_governor">Virginia state legislator and governor</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg"><img alt="Governor\'s Palace" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1520" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/290px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/435px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg/580px-Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Williamsburg_Governors_Palace_Front_Dscn7232.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Governor%27s_Palace_(Williamsburg,_Virginia)" title="Governor\'s Palace (Williamsburg, Virginia)">Governor\'s Palace</a>, Governor Jefferson\'s residence in Williamsburg</div></div></div>\n<p>At the start of the Revolution, Jefferson was a <a href="/wiki/Colonel_(United_States)" title="Colonel (United States)">Colonel</a> and was named commander of the <a href="/wiki/Albemarle_County,_Virginia" title="Albemarle County, Virginia">Albemarle County</a> Militia on September 26, 1775.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[56]</a></sup> He was then elected to the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_House_of_Delegates" title="Virginia House of Delegates">Virginia House of Delegates</a> for Albemarle County in September 1776, when finalizing a state constitution was a priority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_pp._101_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_pp._101-61">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fer26_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fer26-62">[58]</a></sup>\nFor nearly three years, he assisted with the constitution and was especially proud of his Bill for Establishing Religious Freedom, which forbade state support of religious institutions or enforcement of religious doctrine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[59]</a></sup> The bill failed to pass, as did his legislation to disestablish the Anglican church, but both were later revived by <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1778, Jefferson was given the task of revising the state\'s laws. He drafted 126 bills in three years, including laws to streamline the judicial system. Jefferson\'s proposed statutes provided for general education, which he considered the basis of "republican government".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_pp._101_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_pp._101-61">[57]</a></sup> He had become alarmed that Virginia\'s powerful landed gentry were becoming a hereditary aristocracy. He took the lead in abolishing what he called "feudal and unnatural distinctions." He targeted laws such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entail" title="Entail">entail</a> and <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a> by which the oldest son inherited all the land. The <a href="/wiki/Fee_tail" title="Fee tail">entail</a> laws made it perpetual: the one who inherited the land could not sell it, but had to bequeath it to his oldest son. As a result, increasingly large plantations, worked by white tenant farmers and by black slaves, gained in size and wealth and political power in the eastern ("Tidewater") tobacco areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[61]</a></sup> During the Revolutionary era, all such laws were repealed by the states that had them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson was elected <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Virginia" title="Governor of Virginia">governor</a> for one-year terms in 1779 and 1780.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup> He transferred the state capital from Williamsburg to <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Virginia" title="Richmond, Virginia">Richmond</a>, and introduced measures for public education, religious freedom, and revision of inheritance laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During General <a href="/wiki/Benedict_Arnold" title="Benedict Arnold">Benedict Arnold</a>\'s 1781 invasion of Virginia, Jefferson escaped Richmond just ahead of the British forces, and the city was burned to the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[66]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cornwallis,_1st_Marquess_Cornwallis" title="Charles Cornwallis, 1st Marquess Cornwallis">General Charles Cornwallis</a> that spring dispatched a cavalry force led by <a href="/wiki/Banastre_Tarleton" title="Banastre Tarleton">Banastre Tarleton</a> to capture Jefferson and members of the Assembly at Monticello, but <a href="/wiki/Jack_Jouett" title="Jack Jouett">Jack Jouett</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_militia" title="Virginia militia">Virginia militia</a> thwarted the British plan. Jefferson escaped to <a href="/wiki/Poplar_Forest" title="Poplar Forest">Poplar Forest</a>, his plantation to the west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[67]</a></sup> When the General Assembly reconvened in June 1781, it conducted an inquiry into Jefferson\'s actions which eventually concluded that Jefferson had acted with honor&#8212;but he was not re-elected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In April of the same year, his daughter Lucy died at age one. A second daughter of that name was born the following year, but she died at age three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[69]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia"><i>Notes on the State of Virginia</i></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Notes_on_the_State_of_Virginia" title="Notes on the State of Virginia">Notes on the State of Virginia</a></div>\n<p>Jefferson received a letter of inquiry in 1780 about the geography, history, and government of Virginia from French diplomat <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Barb%C3%A9-Marbois" title="Fran&#231;ois Barb&#233;-Marbois">Fran&#231;ois Barb&#233;-Marbois</a>, who was gathering data on the United States. Jefferson included his written responses in a book, <i>Notes on the State of Virginia</i> (1785).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[70]</a></sup> He compiled the book over five years, including reviews of scientific knowledge, Virginia\'s history, politics, laws, culture, and geography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[71]</a></sup> The book explores what constitutes a good society, using Virginia as an exemplar. Jefferson included extensive data about the state\'s natural resources and economy, and wrote at length about slavery, <a href="/wiki/Miscegenation" title="Miscegenation">miscegenation</a>, and his belief that blacks and whites could not live together as free people in one society because of justified resentments of the enslaved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[72]</a></sup> He also wrote of his views on the American Indian and considered them as equals in body and mind to European settlers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Notes</i> was first published in 1785 in French and appeared in English in 1787.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[75]</a></sup> Biographer <a href="/wiki/George_Tucker_(politician)" title="George Tucker (politician)">George Tucker</a> considered the work "surprising in the extent of the information which a single individual had been thus able to acquire, as to the physical features of the state",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[76]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Merrill_D._Peterson" title="Merrill D. Peterson">Merrill D. Peterson</a> described it as an accomplishment for which all Americans should be grateful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[77]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Member_of_Congress">Member of Congress</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg"><img alt="Legislative chamber" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg/220px-Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg/330px-Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg/440px-Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Independence_Hall_Assembly_Room.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Independence Hall Assembly Room where Jefferson served in Congress</div></div></div>\n<p>The United States formed a <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a> following victory in the Revolutionary War and a <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_(1783)" title="Treaty of Paris (1783)">peace treaty</a> with Great Britain in 1783, to which Jefferson was appointed as a Virginia delegate. He was a member of the committee setting foreign exchange rates and <a href="/wiki/Plan_for_Establishing_Uniformity_in_the_Coinage,_Weights,_and_Measures_of_the_United_States" title="Plan for Establishing Uniformity in the Coinage, Weights, and Measures of the United States">recommended</a> an American currency based on the decimal system which was adopted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[78]</a></sup> He advised formation of the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_the_States" title="Committee of the States">Committee of the States</a> to fill the power vacuum when Congress was in recess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[79]</a></sup> The Committee met when Congress adjourned, but disagreements rendered it dysfunctional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Congress\'s 1783&#8211;84 session, Jefferson acted as chairman of committees to establish a viable system of government for the new Republic and to propose a policy for the settlement of the western territories. Jefferson was the principal author of the <a href="/wiki/Land_Ordinance_of_1784" title="Land Ordinance of 1784">Land Ordinance of 1784</a>, whereby Virginia ceded to the national government the vast area that it claimed northwest of the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>. He insisted that this territory should not be used as colonial territory by any of the thirteen states, but that it should be divided into sections which could become states. He plotted borders for nine new states in their initial stages and wrote an ordinance banning slavery in all the nation\'s territories. Congress made extensive revisions, including rejection of the ban on slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peretson189_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peretson189-85">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup> The provisions banning slavery were known later as the "Jefferson Proviso;" they were modified and implemented three years later in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Ordinance" title="Northwest Ordinance">Northwest Ordinance</a> of 1787 and became the law for the entire Northwest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peretson189_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peretson189-85">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minister_to_France">Minister to France</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="Young Thomas Jefferson" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9840" data-file-width="7707" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/440px-Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mather_Brown_-_Thomas_Jefferson_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Thomas Jefferson while in London in 1786 by <a href="/wiki/Mather_Brown" title="Mather Brown">Mather Brown</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1784, Jefferson was sent by the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_Confederation" title="Congress of the Confederation">Congress of the Confederation</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[e]</a></sup> to join Benjamin Franklin and John Adams in Paris as Minister <a href="/wiki/Plenipotentiary" title="Plenipotentiary">Plenipotentiary</a> for Negotiating Treaties of Amity and Commerce with <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Great Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electorate_of_Saxony" title="Electorate of Saxony">Saxony</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Naples" title="Kingdom of Naples">Naples</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sardinia" title="Kingdom of Sardinia">Sardinia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Pope" title="The Pope">The Pope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">Venice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Tuscany" title="Grand Duchy of Tuscany">Tuscany</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sublime_Porte" title="Sublime Porte">Sublime Porte</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algiers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunis</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tripoli" title="Kingdom of Tripoli">Tripoli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[83]</a></sup>  Some believed that the recently widowed Jefferson was depressed and that the assignment would distract him from his wife\'s death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[84]</a></sup> With his young daughter Patsy and two servants, he departed in July 1784, arriving in Paris the next month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stewart39_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart39-90">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[86]</a></sup> Less than a year later he was assigned the additional duty of succeeding Franklin as Minister to France. French foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Charles_Gravier,_comte_de_Vergennes" title="Charles Gravier, comte de Vergennes">Count de Vergennes</a> commented, "You replace Monsieur Franklin, I hear." Jefferson replied, "I <i>succeed</i>. No man can replace him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[87]</a></sup> During his five years in Paris Jefferson played a leading role in shaping the foreign policy of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson had Patsy educated at the <a href="/wiki/Pentemont_Abbey" title="Pentemont Abbey">Pentemont Abbey</a>. In 1786, he met and fell in love with <a href="/wiki/Maria_Cosway" title="Maria Cosway">Maria Cosway</a>, an accomplished&#8212;and married&#8212;Italian-English musician of 27. They saw each other frequently over a period of six weeks. She returned to Great Britain, but they maintained a lifelong correspondence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[89]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson sent for his youngest surviving child, nine-year-old Polly, in June 1787, who was accompanied on her voyage by a young slave from Monticello, <a href="/wiki/Sally_Hemings" title="Sally Hemings">Sally Hemings</a>. Jefferson had taken her older brother <a href="/wiki/James_Hemings" title="James Hemings">James Hemings</a> to Paris as part of his domestic staff, and had him trained in French cuisine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup> According to Sally\'s son, <a href="/wiki/Madison_Hemings" title="Madison Hemings">Madison Hemings</a>, the 16-year-old Sally and Jefferson began a sexual relationship in Paris, where she became pregnant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-madisonstatement_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-madisonstatement-96">[91]</a></sup> According to his account, Hemings agreed to return to the United States only after Jefferson promised to free her children when they came of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-madisonstatement_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-madisonstatement-96">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While in France, Jefferson became a regular companion of the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a>, a <a href="/wiki/France_in_the_American_Revolutionary_War" title="France in the American Revolutionary War">French hero of the American Revolutionary War</a>, and Jefferson used his influence to procure trade agreements with France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowers328_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowers328-97">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burstein120_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burstein120-98">[93]</a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> began, Jefferson allowed his Paris residence, the <a href="/wiki/H%C3%B4tel_de_Langeac" title="H&#244;tel de Langeac">H&#244;tel de Langeac</a>, to be used for meetings by Lafayette and other republicans. He was in Paris during the storming of the Bastille<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup> and consulted with Lafayette while the latter drafted the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_the_Rights_of_Man_and_of_the_Citizen" title="Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen">Declaration of the Rights of Man and of the Citizen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup> Jefferson often found his mail opened by postmasters, so he invented his own enciphering device, the "<a href="/wiki/Jefferson_disk" title="Jefferson disk">Wheel Cipher</a>"; he wrote important communications in code for the rest of his career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[f]</a></sup> Jefferson left Paris for America in September 1789, intending to return soon; however, President <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a> appointed him the country\'s first Secretary of State, forcing him to remain in the nation\'s capitol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> Jefferson remained a firm supporter of the French Revolution, while opposing its more violent elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secretary_of_State">Secretary of State</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">First Party System</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg"><img alt="Thomas Jefferson" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="506" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/200px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg/300px-T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/46/T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:T_Jefferson_by_Charles_Willson_Peale_1791_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thomas Jefferson in 1791 at 49 by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Willson_Peale" title="Charles Willson Peale">Charles Willson Peale</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Soon after returning from France, Jefferson accepted Washington\'s invitation to serve as <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup> Pressing issues at this time were the national debt and the permanent location of the capital. Jefferson opposed a national debt, preferring that each state retire its own, in contrast to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of the Treasury</a> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a>, who desired consolidation of various states\' debts by the federal government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> Hamilton also had bold plans to establish the national credit and a national bank, but Jefferson strenuously opposed this and attempted to undermine his agenda, which nearly led Washington to dismiss him from his cabinet. Jefferson later left the cabinet voluntarily; Washington never forgave him, and never spoke to him again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The second major issue was the capital\'s permanent location. Hamilton favored a capital close to the major commercial centers of the Northeast, while Washington, Jefferson, and other agrarians wanted it located to the south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke,_1970,_pp._523&#8211;45_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke,_1970,_pp._523&#8211;45-108">[102]</a></sup> After lengthy deadlock, the <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1790" title="Compromise of 1790">Compromise of 1790</a> was struck, permanently locating the capital on the Potomac River, and the federal government assumed the war debts of all thirteen states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooke,_1970,_pp._523&#8211;45_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cooke,_1970,_pp._523&#8211;45-108">[102]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Spring of 1791, Jefferson and Congressman James Madison took a vacation to <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>. Jefferson had been suffering from migraines and he was tired of Hamilton in-fighting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randall_1996_p1_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Randall_1996_p1-109">[103]</a></sup> In May 1792, Jefferson was alarmed at the political rivalries taking shape; he wrote to Washington, urging him to run for re-election that year as a unifying influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup> He urged the president to rally the citizenry to a party that would defend democracy against the corrupting influence of banks and monied interests, as espoused by the Federalists. Historians recognize this letter as the earliest delineation of <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup> Jefferson, Madison, and other Democratic-Republican organizers favored <a href="/wiki/States%27_rights" title="States\' rights">states\' rights</a> and local control and opposed federal concentration of power, whereas Hamilton sought more power for the federal government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson supported France against Britain when the two nations fought in 1793, though his arguments in the Cabinet were undercut by French Revolutionary envoy <a href="/wiki/Edmond-Charles_Gen%C3%AAt" title="Edmond-Charles Gen&#234;t">Edmond-Charles Gen&#234;t</a>\'s open scorn for President Washington.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup> In his discussions with British Minister <a href="/wiki/George_Hammond_(diplomat)" title="George Hammond (diplomat)">George Hammond</a>, Jefferson tried unsuccessfully to persuade the British to acknowledge their violation of the Treaty of Paris, to vacate their posts in the Northwest, and to compensate the U.S. for slaves whom the British had freed at the end of the war. Seeking a return to private life, Jefferson resigned the cabinet position in December 1793, perhaps to bolster his political influence from outside the administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the Washington administration negotiated the <a href="/wiki/Jay_Treaty" title="Jay Treaty">Jay Treaty</a> with Great Britain (1794), Jefferson saw a cause around which to rally his party and organized a national opposition from Monticello.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup> The treaty, designed by Hamilton, aimed to reduce tensions and increase trade. Jefferson warned that it would increase British influence and subvert republicanism, calling it "the boldest act [Hamilton and Jay] ever ventured on to undermine the government".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup> The Treaty passed, but it expired in 1805 during Jefferson\'s administration and was not renewed. Jefferson continued his pro-French stance; during the violence of the <a href="/wiki/Reign_of_Terror" title="Reign of Terror">Reign of Terror</a>, he declined to disavow the revolution: "To back away from France would be to undermine the cause of republicanism in America."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1796_and_Vice_Presidency">Election of 1796 and Vice Presidency</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1796" title="United States presidential election, 1796">United States presidential election, 1796</a> and <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1796.svg"><img alt="Electoral College map" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ElectoralCollege1796.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1796.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ElectoralCollege1796.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1796.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/ElectoralCollege1796.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1796.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1796.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1796 election results</div></div></div>\n<p>In the presidential campaign of 1796, Jefferson lost the electoral college vote to Federalist John Adams by 71&#8211;68 and was elected vice president because of a mistake in voting for Adams\'s running mate. As presiding officer of the Senate, he assumed a more passive role than his predecessor John Adams. He allowed the Senate to freely conduct debates and confined his participation to procedural issues, which he called an "honorable and easy" role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup> Jefferson had previously studied parliamentary law and procedure for 40 years, making him unusually well qualified to serve as presiding officer. In 1800, he published his assembled notes on Senate procedure as <i><a href="/wiki/Jefferson%27s_Manual" title="Jefferson\'s Manual">A Manual of Parliamentary Practice</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson held four confidential talks with French consul Joseph L&#233;tombe in the spring of 1797 where he attacked Adams, predicting that his rival would serve only one term. He also encouraged France to invade England, and advised L&#233;tombe to stall any American envoys sent to Paris by instructing him to "listen to them and then drag out the negotiations at length and mollify them by the urbanity of the proceedings."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup> This toughened the tone that the French government adopted toward the Adams administration. After Adams\'s initial peace envoys were rebuffed, Jefferson and his supporters lobbied for the release of papers related to the incident, called the <a href="/wiki/XYZ_Affair" title="XYZ Affair">XYZ Affair</a> after the letters used to disguise the identities of the French officials involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> However, the tactic backfired when it was revealed that French officials had demanded bribes, rallying public support against France. The U.S. began an undeclared naval war with France known as the <a href="/wiki/Quasi-War" title="Quasi-War">Quasi-War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the Adams presidency, the Federalists rebuilt the military, levied new taxes, and enacted the <a href="/wiki/Alien_and_Sedition_Acts" title="Alien and Sedition Acts">Alien and Sedition Acts</a>. Jefferson believed that these laws were intended to suppress Democratic-Republicans, rather than prosecute enemy aliens, and considered them unconstitutional.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[117]</a></sup> To rally opposition, he and James Madison anonymously wrote the <a href="/wiki/Kentucky_and_Virginia_Resolutions" title="Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions">Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions</a>, declaring that the federal government had no right to exercise powers not specifically delegated to it by the states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup> The resolutions followed the "<a href="/wiki/Interposition" title="Interposition">interposition</a>" approach of Madison, in which states may shield their citizens from federal laws that they deem unconstitutional. Jefferson advocated <a href="/wiki/Nullification_(U.S._Constitution)" title="Nullification (U.S. Constitution)">nullification</a>, allowing states to invalidate federal laws altogether.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[g]</a></sup> Jefferson warned that, "unless arrested at the threshold", the Alien and Sedition Acts would "necessarily drive these states into revolution and blood".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[121]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Ron_Chernow" title="Ron Chernow">Ron Chernow</a> claims that "the theoretical damage of the Kentucky and Virginia Resolutions was deep and lasting, and was a recipe for disunion", contributing to the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> as well as later events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[122]</a></sup> Washington was so appalled by the resolutions that he told Patrick Henry that, if "systematically and pertinaciously pursued", the resolutions would "dissolve the union or produce coercion."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chernow587_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow587-130">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson and Madison moved to Philadelphia and founded the <a href="/wiki/National_Gazette" title="National Gazette">National Gazette</a> in 1791, along with poet and writer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phillip_Freneau" title="Phillip Freneau">Phillip Freneau</a>, in an effort to counter Hamilton\'s Federalist policies, which Hamilton was promoting through the influential Federalist newspaper the <a href="/wiki/Gazette_of_the_United_States" title="Gazette of the United States">Gazette of the United States</a>. The National Gazette made particular criticism of the policies promoted by Alexander Hamilton, often through anonymous essays signed by the pen name <i>Brutus</i> at Jefferson\'s urging, which were actually written by Madison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[124]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson had always admired Washington\'s leadership skills but felt that his Federalist party was leading the country in the wrong direction. Jefferson thought it wise not to attend his funeral in 1799 because of acute differences with Washington while serving as Secretary of State, and remained at Monticello.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[125]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Election_of_1800">Election of 1800</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1800.svg"><img alt="Electoral College map" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="446" data-file-width="542" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/ElectoralCollege1800.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1800.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1800.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1800 election results</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1800" title="United States presidential election, 1800">United States presidential election, 1800</a></div>\n<p>In the 1800 presidential election, Jefferson contended once more against Federalist John Adams.
