Adams\'s campaign was weakened by unpopular taxes and vicious Federalist infighting over his actions in the Quasi-War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[126]</a></sup> Republicans pointed to the Alien and Sedition Acts and accused the Federalists of being secret monarchists, while Federalists charged that Jefferson was a godless libertine in thrall to the French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[127]</a></sup> Historian Joyce Appleby said the election was "one of the most acrimonious in the annals of American history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[128]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Republicans ultimately won more electoral college votes, but Jefferson and his vice presidential candidate <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a> unexpectedly received an equal total. Due to the tie, the election was decided by the Federalist-dominated House of Representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[h]</a></sup> Hamilton lobbied Federalist representatives on Jefferson\'s behalf, believing him a lesser political evil than Burr. On February 17, 1801, after thirty-six ballots, the House elected Jefferson president and Burr vice president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W284_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-W284-137">[130]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The win was marked by Republican celebrations throughout the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[131]</a></sup> Some of Jefferson\'s opponents argued that he owed his victory over Adams to the South\'s inflated number of electors, due to counting slaves as partial population under the <a href="/wiki/Three-Fifths_Compromise" title="Three-Fifths Compromise">Three-Fifths Compromise</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[132]</a></sup> Others alleged that Jefferson secured <a href="/wiki/James_A._Bayard_(elder)" title="James A. Bayard (elder)">James Asheton Bayard</a>\'s tie-breaking electoral vote by guaranteeing the retention of various Federalist posts in the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W284_137-2"><a href="#cite_note-W284-137">[130]</a></sup> Jefferson disputed the allegation, and the historical record is inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[133]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The transition proceeded smoothly, marking a watershed in American history. As historian <a href="/wiki/Gordon_S._Wood" title="Gordon S. Wood">Gordon S. Wood</a> writes, "it was one of the first popular elections in modern history that resulted in the peaceful transfer of power from one \'party\' to another."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W284_137-3"><a href="#cite_note-W284-137">[130]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Presidency_.281801.E2.80.931809.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_(1801&#8211;1809)">Presidency (1801&#8211;1809)</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Thomas_Jefferson" title="Presidency of Thomas Jefferson">Presidency of Thomas Jefferson</a></div>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:auto;text-align:left;line-height:1.2em;margin-left:1em; margin-right:0; float:right; clear:right;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="3" style="line-height:1.5em;font-size:110%;background:#DCDCDC;text-align:center">The Jefferson Cabinet</th></tr><tr><th>Office</th><th>Name</th><th>Term</th></tr><tr><td colspan="3" style="background:#000"></td></tr><tr><td><a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a></td><th style="font-weight:bold">Thomas Jefferson</th><td>1801&#8211;1809</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" style="background:#D1D1D1"></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Vice_President_of_the_United_States" title="Vice President of the United States">Vice President</a></td><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a></th><td>1801&#8211;1805</td></tr><tr class="mw-empty-elt"></tr><tr><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(vice_president)" title="George Clinton (vice president)">George Clinton</a></th><td>1805&#8211;1809</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" style="background:#D1D1D1"></td></tr><tr><td><a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a></td><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">James Madison</a></th><td>1801&#8211;1809</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" style="background:#D1D1D1"></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Secretary of the Treasury">Secretary of Treasury</a></td><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/Samuel_Dexter" title="Samuel Dexter">Samuel Dexter</a></th><td>1801</td></tr><tr class="mw-empty-elt"></tr><tr><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a></th><td>1801&#8211;1809</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" style="background:#D1D1D1"></td></tr><tr><td><a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a></td><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/Henry_Dearborn" title="Henry Dearborn">Henry Dearborn</a></th><td>1801&#8211;1809</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" style="background:#D1D1D1"></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="3"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Attorney_General" title="United States Attorney General">Attorney General</a></td><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/Levi_Lincoln_Sr." title="Levi Lincoln Sr.">Levi Lincoln Sr.</a></th><td>1801&#8211;1804</td></tr><tr class="mw-empty-elt"></tr><tr><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/John_Breckinridge_(U.S._Attorney_General)" title="John Breckinridge (U.S. Attorney General)">John Breckinridge</a></th><td>1805&#8211;1806</td></tr><tr class="mw-empty-elt"></tr><tr><th style="font-weight:bold"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar_A._Rodney" title="Caesar A. Rodney">Caesar A. Rodney</a></th><td>1807&#8211;1809</td></tr><tr><td colspan="3" style="background:#D1D1D1"></td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2"><a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="United States Secretary of the Navy">Secretary of the Navy</a></td><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Stoddert" title="Benjamin Stoddert">Benjamin Stoddert</a></th><td>1801</td></tr><tr class="mw-empty-elt"></tr><tr><th style="font-weight:bold"><a href="/wiki/Robert_Smith_(Cabinet_member)" title="Robert Smith (Cabinet member)">Robert Smith</a></th><td>1801&#8211;1809</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Jefferson was sworn in by <a href="/wiki/Chief_Justice_of_the_United_States" title="Chief Justice of the United States">Chief Justice</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a> at the new Capitol in Washington, D.C. on March 4, 1801. In contrast to his predecessors, Jefferson exhibited a dislike of formal etiquette; he arrived alone on horseback without escort, dressed plainly<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[134]</a></sup> and, after dismounting, retired his own horse to the nearby stable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[135]</a></sup> His inaugural address struck a note of reconciliation, declaring, "We have been called by different names brethren of the same principle. We are all Republicans, we are all Federalists."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[136]</a></sup> Ideologically, Jefferson stressed "equal and exact justice to all men", minority rights, and freedom of speech, religion, and press.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p40_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p40-145">[137]</a></sup> He said that a free and democratic government was "the strongest government on earth."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p40_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p40-145">[137]</a></sup> He nominated moderate Republicans to his cabinet: James Madison as Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dearborn" title="Henry Dearborn">Henry Dearborn</a> as Secretary of War, <a href="/wiki/Levi_Lincoln_Sr." title="Levi Lincoln Sr.">Levi Lincoln</a> as Attorney General, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Smith_(Cabinet_member)" title="Robert Smith (Cabinet member)">Robert Smith</a> as Secretary of the Navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson41_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson41-146">[138]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Upon assuming office, he first confronted an $83 million national debt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham387_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham387-147">[139]</a></sup> He began dismantling Hamilton\'s Federalist fiscal system with help from Secretary of Treasury <a href="/wiki/Albert_Gallatin" title="Albert Gallatin">Albert Gallatin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson41_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson41-146">[138]</a></sup> Jefferson\'s administration eliminated the whiskey excise and other taxes after closing "unnecessary offices" and cutting "useless establishments and expenses".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood,_2010,_p._293_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood,_2010,_p._293-148">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[141]</a></sup> They attempted to disassemble the national bank and its effect of increasing national debt, but were dissuaded by Gallatin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[142]</a></sup> Jefferson shrank the Navy, deeming it unnecessary in peacetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[143]</a></sup> Instead, he incorporated a fleet of inexpensive gunboats used only for defense with the idea that they would not provoke foreign hostilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood,_2010,_p._293_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wood,_2010,_p._293-148">[140]</a></sup> After two terms, he had lowered the national debt from $83 million to $57 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham387_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham387-147">[139]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson pardoned several of those imprisoned under the Alien and Sedition Acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[144]</a></sup> Congressional Republicans repealed the <a href="/wiki/Midnight_Judges_Act" title="Midnight Judges Act">Judiciary Act of 1801</a>, which removed nearly all of Adams\'s "midnight judges" from office. A subsequent appointment battle led to the Supreme Court\'s landmark decision in <i><a href="/wiki/Marbury_v._Madison" title="Marbury v. Madison">Marbury v. Madison</a></i>, asserting judicial review over executive branch actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham,_2012,_p._413_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham,_2012,_p._413-153">[145]</a></sup> Jefferson appointed three <a href="/wiki/Associate_Justice_of_the_Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Associate Justice of the Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court justices</a>: <a href="/wiki/William_Johnson_(judge)" title="William Johnson (judge)">William Johnson</a> (1804), <a href="/wiki/Henry_Brockholst_Livingston" title="Henry Brockholst Livingston">Henry Brockholst Livingston</a> (1807), and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Todd" title="Thomas Todd">Thomas Todd</a> (1807).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[146]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson strongly felt the need for a national military university, producing an officer engineering corps for a national defense based on the advancement of the sciences, rather than having to rely on foreign sources for top grade engineers with questionable loyalty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[147]</a></sup> He signed the <a href="/wiki/Military_Peace_Establishment_Act" title="Military Peace Establishment Act">Military Peace Establishment Act</a> on March 16, 1802, thus founding the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Military_Academy" title="United States Military Academy">United States Military Academy</a> at West Point. The Act documented in 29 sections a new set of laws and limits for the military. Jefferson was also hoping to bring reform to the Executive branch, replacing Federalists and active opponents throughout the officer corps to promote Republican values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[148]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_Barbary_War">First Barbary War</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First_Barbary_War" title="First Barbary War">First Barbary War</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg"><img alt="Map. Barbary Coast of North Africa 1806" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="1247" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg/360px-1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg/540px-1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg/720px-1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1800_map_Afrique_by_Arrowsmith_BPL_15210_detail2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Barbary Coast of North Africa 1806. Left is Morocco at Gibraltar, center is Tunis, and right is Tripoli.</div></div></div>\n<p>American merchant ships had been protected from <a href="/wiki/Barbary_Coast" title="Barbary Coast">Barbary Coast</a> pirates by the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> when the states were British colonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[149]</a></sup> After independence, however, pirates often captured U.S. merchant ships, pillaged cargoes, and enslaved or held crew members for ransom. Jefferson had opposed paying tribute to the Barbary States since 1785.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnes36_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Barnes36-158">[150]</a></sup>\nIn March 1786, he and John Adams went to London to negotiate with Tripoli\'s envoy, ambassador Sidi Haji Abdrahaman (or Sidi Haji Abdul Rahman Adja).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[151]</a></sup>\nIn 1801, he authorized a U.S. Navy fleet under Commodore <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dale" title="Richard Dale">Richard Dale</a> to make a show of force in the Mediterranean, the first American naval squadron to cross the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham_pp._364_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham_pp._364-160">[152]</a></sup> Following the fleet\'s first engagement, he successfully asked Congress for a declaration of war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham_pp._364_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham_pp._364-160">[152]</a></sup> The subsequent "First Barbary War" was the first foreign war fought by the U.S.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[153]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pasha" title="Pasha">Pasha</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a> <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_Karamanli" title="Yusuf Karamanli">Yusuf Karamanli</a> captured the <a href="/wiki/USS_Philadelphia_(1799)" title="USS Philadelphia (1799)">USS&#160;<i>Philadelphia</i></a>, so Jefferson authorized <a href="/wiki/William_Eaton_(soldier)" title="William Eaton (soldier)">William Eaton</a>, the U.S. Consul to <a href="/wiki/Tunis" title="Tunis">Tunis</a>, to lead a force to restore the pasha\'s older brother to the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[154]</a></sup> The American navy forced Tunis and <a href="/wiki/Algiers" title="Algiers">Algiers</a> into breaking their alliance with Tripoli. Jefferson ordered five separate naval bombardments of Tripoli, leading the pasha to sign a treaty that restored peace in the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[155]</a></sup> This victory proved only temporary, but according to Wood, "many Americans celebrated it as a vindication of their policy of spreading free trade around the world and as a great victory for liberty over tyranny."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[156]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Louisiana_Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Purchase" title="Louisiana Purchase">Louisiana Purchase</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_Purchase.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2480" data-file-width="3507" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Louisiana_Purchase.png/360px-Louisiana_Purchase.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Louisiana_Purchase.png/540px-Louisiana_Purchase.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Louisiana_Purchase.png/720px-Louisiana_Purchase.png 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louisiana_Purchase.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 1803 Louisiana Purchase totaled 827,987 <a href="/wiki/Square_mile" title="Square mile">square miles</a> (2,144,480 square kilometers), doubling the size of the United States.</div></div></div>\n<p>Spain <a href="/wiki/Third_Treaty_of_San_Ildefonso" title="Third Treaty of San Ildefonso">ceded ownership</a> of the Louisiana territory in 1800 to the more predominant France. Jefferson was greatly concerned that <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a>\'s broad interests in the vast territory would threaten the security of the continent and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> shipping. He wrote that the cession "works most sorely on the U.S. It completely reverses all the political relations of the U.S."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[157]</a></sup> In 1802, he instructed <a href="/wiki/James_Monroe" title="James Monroe">James Monroe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_R._Livingston_(chancellor)" title="Robert R. Livingston (chancellor)">Robert R. Livingston</a> to negotiate with Napoleon to purchase <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> and adjacent coastal areas from France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[158]</a></sup> In early 1803, Jefferson offered Napoleon nearly $10 million for 40,000 square miles (100,000 square kilometers) of tropical territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freehling2005_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freehling2005-167">[159]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Napoleon realized that French military control was impractical over such a vast remote territory, and he was in dire need of funds for his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_United_Kingdom_in_the_Napoleonic_Wars" title="The United Kingdom in the Napoleonic Wars">wars on the home front</a>. In early April 1803, he unexpectedly made negotiators a counter-offer to sell 827,987 square miles (2,144,480 square kilometers) of French territory for $15 million, doubling the size of the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freehling2005_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freehling2005-167">[159]</a></sup> U.S. negotiators seized this unique opportunity and accepted the offer and signed the treaty on April 30, 1803.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham387_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham387-147">[139]</a></sup> Word of the unexpected purchase didn\'t reach Jefferson until July 3, 1803.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham387_147-3"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham387-147">[139]</a></sup> He unknowingly acquired the most fertile tract of land of its size on Earth, making the new country self-sufficient in food and other resources. The sale also significantly curtailed British and French imperial ambitions in North America, removing obstacles to U.S. westward expansion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis208_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis208-168">[160]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most thought that this was an exceptional opportunity, despite Republican reservations about the Constitutional authority of the federal government to acquire land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Rise_of_American_Democracy_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Rise_of_American_Democracy-169">[161]</a></sup> Jefferson initially thought that a Constitutional <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Senate_changes" title="History of the United States Constitution">amendment</a> was necessary to purchase and govern the new territory; but he later changed his mind, fearing that this would give cause to oppose the purchase, and he therefore urged a speedy debate and ratification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[162]</a></sup> On October 20, 1803, the Senate ratified the purchase treaty by a vote of 24&#8211;7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[163]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the purchase, Jefferson preserved the region\'s Spanish legal code and instituted a gradual approach for integrating settlers into American democracy. He believed that a period of federal rule would be necessary while Louisianians adjusted to their new nation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[i]</a></sup> Historians have differed in their assessments regarding the constitutional implications of the sale,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[166]</a></sup> but they typically hail the Louisiana acquisition as a major accomplishment. <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Jackson_Turner" title="Frederick Jackson Turner">Frederick Jackson Turner</a> called the purchase the most formative event in American history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis208_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis208-168">[160]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lewis_and_Clark_expedition">Lewis and Clark expedition</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Lewis and Clark Expedition">Lewis and Clark Expedition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Expedition_(1806)" title="Red River Expedition (1806)">Red River Expedition (1806)</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pike_expedition" title="Pike expedition">Pike expedition</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg"><img alt="Corps of Discover on river boat October 1805" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2346" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg/220px-Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg/330px-Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg/440px-Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lewis_and_clark-expedition.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Corps of Discovery, October 1805</div></div></div>\n<p>Jefferson anticipated further westward settlements due to the Louisiana Purchase and arranged for the exploration and mapping of the uncharted territory. He sought to establish a U.S. claim ahead of competing European interests and to find the rumored <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ambrose76_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ambrose76-176">[167]</a></sup> Jefferson and others were influenced by exploration accounts of <a href="/wiki/Antoine-Simon_Le_Page_du_Pratz" title="Antoine-Simon Le Page du Pratz">Le Page du Pratz</a> in Louisiana (1763) and Captain <a href="/wiki/James_Cook" title="James Cook">James Cook</a> in the Pacific (1784),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[168]</a></sup> and they persuaded Congress in 1804 to fund an expedition to explore and <a href="/wiki/File:Lewis_and_Clark_Expidition_Map.jpg" title="File:Lewis and Clark Expidition Map.jpg">map</a> the newly acquired territory to the Pacific Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[169]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson appointed <a href="/wiki/Meriwether_Lewis" title="Meriwether Lewis">Meriwether Lewis</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Clark" title="William Clark">William Clark</a> to be leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Corps_of_Discovery" title="Corps of Discovery">Corps of Discovery</a> (1803&#8211;1806).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[170]</a></sup> In the months leading up to the expedition, Jefferson tutored Lewis in the sciences of mapping, botany, natural history, mineralogy, and astronomy and navigation, giving him unlimited access to his library at Monticello, which included the largest collection of books in the world on the subject of the geography and natural history of the North American continent, along with an impressive collection of maps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[171]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The expedition lasted from May 1804 to September 1806 <a href="/wiki/Template:Timeline_of_the_Lewis_and_Clark_Expedition" title="Template:Timeline of the Lewis and Clark Expedition">(see Timeline)</a> and obtained a wealth of scientific and geographic knowledge, including knowledge of many Indian tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[172]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dt><big>Other expeditions</big></dt></dl>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Expedition_(1806)" title="Red River Expedition (1806)">Red River Expedition (1806)</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pike_expedition" title="Pike expedition">Pike expedition</a></div>\n<p>In addition to the Corps of Discovery, Jefferson organized three other western expeditions: the <a href="/wiki/William_Dunbar_(explorer)" title="William Dunbar (explorer)">William Dunbar</a> and George Hunter expedition on the <a href="/wiki/Ouachita_River" title="Ouachita River">Ouachita River</a> (1804&#8211;1805), the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Expedition_(1806)" title="Red River Expedition (1806)">Thomas Freeman and Peter Custis expedition</a> (1806) on the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_South" title="Red River of the South">Red River</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pike_expedition" title="Pike expedition">Zebulon Pike expedition</a> (1806&#8211;1807) into the Rocky Mountains and the Southwest. All three produced valuable information about the American frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[173]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Indian_policies">American Indian policies</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_and_Native_Americans" title="Thomas Jefferson and Native Americans">Thomas Jefferson and Native Americans</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Black_Hoof.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="412" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Black_Hoof.jpg/180px-Black_Hoof.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Black_Hoof.jpg/270px-Black_Hoof.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Black_Hoof.jpg/360px-Black_Hoof.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Black_Hoof.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Black Hoof, leader of the Shawnee, accepted Jefferson\'s Indian assimilation policies.</div></div></div>\n<p>Jefferson\'s experiences with the American Indians began during his boyhood in Virginia and extended through his political career and into his retirement. He refuted the contemporary notion that Indians were an inferior people and maintained that they were equal in body and mind to people of European descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[174]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As governor of Virginia during the Revolutionary War, Jefferson recommended moving the <a href="/wiki/Cherokee" title="Cherokee">Cherokee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shawnee" title="Shawnee">Shawnee</a> tribes, who had allied with the British, to west of the Mississippi River. But when he took office as president, he quickly took measures to avert another major conflict, as American and Indian societies were in collision and the British were inciting Indian tribes from Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller2008_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller2008-184">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheehan1974_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan1974-185">[176]</a></sup> In Georgia, he stipulated that the state would release its legal claims for lands to its west in exchange for military support in expelling the Cherokee from Georgia. This facilitated his policy of western expansion, to "advance compactly as we multiply".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[177]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In keeping with his <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> thinking, President Jefferson adopted an assimilation policy towards American Indians known as his "civilization program" which included securing peaceful U.S. <b>&#8211;</b> Indian treaty alliances and encouraging agriculture. Jefferson advocated that Indian tribes should make federal purchases by credit holding their lands as collateral for repayment. Various tribes accepted Jefferson\'s policies, including the Shawnees led by <a href="/wiki/Black_Hoof" title="Black Hoof">Black Hoof</a>, the Creek, and the Cherokees. However, some Shawnees broke off from Black Hoof, led by <a href="/wiki/Tecumseh" title="Tecumseh">Tecumseh</a>, and opposed Jefferson\'s assimilation policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[178]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historian Bernard Sheehan argues that Jefferson believed that assimilation was best for American Indians; second best was removal to the west. He felt that the worst outcome of the cultural and resources conflict between American citizens and American Indians would be their attacking the whites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheehan1974_185-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan1974-185">[176]</a></sup> Jefferson told Secretary of War General <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dearborn" title="Henry Dearborn">Henry Dearborn</a> (Indian affairs were then under the War Department), "If we are constrained to lift the hatchet against any tribe, we will never lay it down until that tribe is exterminated or driven beyond the Mississippi."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[179]</a></sup> Miller agrees that Jefferson believed that Indians should assimilate to American customs and agriculture. Historians such as <a href="/wiki/Peter_S._Onuf" title="Peter S. Onuf">Peter S. Onuf</a> and Merrill D. Peterson argue that Jefferson\'s actual Indian policies did little to promote assimilation and were a pretext to seize lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[180]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-election_in_1804_and_second_term">Re-election in 1804 and second term</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1804" title="United States presidential election, 1804">United States presidential election, 1804</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1804.svg"><img alt="Electoral College map" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="593" data-file-width="577" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ElectoralCollege1804.svg/220px-ElectoralCollege1804.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ElectoralCollege1804.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1804.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ElectoralCollege1804.svg/440px-ElectoralCollege1804.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1804.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1804 Electoral College vote</div></div></div>\n<p>Jefferson\'s successful first term occasioned his re-nomination for president by the Republican party, with <a href="/wiki/George_Clinton_(vice_president)" title="George Clinton (vice president)">George Clinton</a> replacing Burr as his running mate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham405_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham405-190">[181]</a></sup> The Federalist party ran <a href="/wiki/Charles_Cotesworth_Pinckney" title="Charles Cotesworth Pinckney">Charles Cotesworth Pinckney</a> of South Carolina, John Adams\'s vice presidential candidate in the 1800 election. The Jefferson-Clinton ticket won overwhelmingly in the electoral college vote, by 162 to 14, promoting their achievement of a strong economy, lower taxes, and the Louisiana Purchase.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham405_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham405-190">[181]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 1806, a split developed in the Republican party, led by fellow Virginian and former Republican ally <a href="/wiki/John_Randolph_of_Roanoke" title="John Randolph of Roanoke">John Randolph</a> who viciously accused President Jefferson on the floor of the House of moving too far in the Federalist direction. In so doing, Randolph permanently set himself apart <a href="/wiki/Tertium_quids" title="Tertium quids">politically</a> from Jefferson. Jefferson and Madison had backed resolutions to limit or ban British imports in retaliation for British actions against American shipping. Also, in 1808, Jefferson was the first president to propose a broad Federal plan to build roads and canals across several states, asking for $20 million, further alarming Randolph and believers of limited government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[182]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson\'s popularity further suffered in his second term due to his response to wars in Europe. Positive relations with Great Britain had diminished, due partly to the antipathy between Jefferson and British diplomat <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Merry" title="Anthony Merry">Anthony Merry</a>. After Napoleon\'s decisive victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz" title="Battle of Austerlitz">Battle of Austerlitz</a> in 1805, Napoleon became more aggressive in his negotiations over trading rights, which American efforts failed to counter. Jefferson then led the enactment of the <a href="/wiki/Embargo_Act_of_1807" title="Embargo Act of 1807">Embargo Act of 1807</a>, directed at both France and Great Britain. This triggered economic chaos in the U.S. and was strongly criticized at the time, resulting in Jefferson having to abandon the policy a year later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[183]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the revolutionary era, the states abolished the international slave trade, but South Carolina reopened it. In his annual message of December 1806, Jefferson denounced the "violations of human rights" attending the international slave trade, calling on the newly elected Congress to criminalize it immediately. In 1807, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Act_Prohibiting_Importation_of_Slaves" title="Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves">Act Prohibiting Importation of Slaves</a>, which Jefferson signed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Randal583_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Randal583-194">[185]</a></sup> The act established severe punishment against the international slave trade, although it did not address the issue domestically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[186]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the wake of the Louisiana Purchase, Jefferson sought to annex Florida from Spain, as brokered by Napoleon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson2002p49_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2002p49-196">[187]</a></sup> Congress agreed to the President\'s request to secretly appropriate purchase money in the "$2,000,000 Bill".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson2002p49_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2002p49-196">[187]</a></sup> The Congressional funding drew criticism from Randolph, who believed that the money would wind up in the coffers of Napoleon. The bill was signed into law; however, negotiations for the project failed. Jefferson lost clout among fellow Republicans, and his use of unofficial Congressional channels was sharply criticized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson2002p49_196-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson2002p49-196">[187]</a></sup> In Haiti, Jefferson\'s neutrality had allowed arms to enable the slave independence movement during its <a href="/wiki/Haitian_Revolution" title="Haitian Revolution">Revolution</a>, and blocked attempts to assist Napoleon, who was defeated there in 1803.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jstorhaiti_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-jstorhaiti-197">[188]</a></sup> But he refused official recognition of the country during his second term, in deference to southern complaints about the racial violence against slave-holders; it was eventually extended to Haiti in 1862.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[189]</a></sup> Domestically, Jefferson\'s grandson James Madison Randolph became the first child born in the White House in 1806.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[190]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burr_conspiracy_and_trial">Burr conspiracy and trial</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Burr%E2%80%93Hamilton_duel" title="Burr&#8211;Hamilton duel">Burr&#8211;Hamilton duel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burr_conspiracy" title="Burr conspiracy">Burr conspiracy</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2206" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg/180px-Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg/270px-Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg/360px-Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vanderlyn_Burr.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aaron Burr<br/>Vanderlyn, 1802</div></div></div>\n<p>Following the 1801 electoral deadlock, Jefferson\'s relationship with his vice president, former New York Senator <a href="/wiki/Aaron_Burr" title="Aaron Burr">Aaron Burr</a>, rapidly eroded. Jefferson suspected Burr of seeking the presidency for himself, while Burr was angered by Jefferson\'s refusal to appoint some of his supporters to federal office. Burr was dropped from the Republican ticket in 1804.\n</p><p>The same year, Burr was soundly defeated in his bid to be elected New York governor. During the campaign, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">Alexander Hamilton</a> publicly made callous remarks regarding Burr\'s moral character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chernow04_p714_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chernow04_p714-200">[191]</a></sup> Subsequently, Burr challenged Hamilton to a duel, mortally wounding him on July 11, 1804. Burr was indicted for Hamilton\'s murder in New York and New Jersey, causing him to flee to Georgia, although he remained President of the Senate during Supreme Court Justice <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Chase" title="Samuel Chase">Samuel Chase</a>\'s impeachment trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[192]</a></sup> Both indictments quietly died and Burr was not prosecuted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_p34_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_p34-202">[193]</a></sup> Also during the election, certain New England separatists approached Burr, desiring a New England federation and intimating that he would be their leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_pp34-35_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_pp34-35-203">[194]</a></sup> However, nothing came of the plot, since Burr had lost the election and his reputation was ruined after killing Hamilton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_pp34-35_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_pp34-35-203">[194]</a></sup> In August 1804, Burr contacted British Minister <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Merry" title="Anthony Merry">Anthony Merry</a> offering to capture U.S. western territory in return for money and British ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Burr_Conspiracy_2000_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Burr_Conspiracy_2000-204">[195]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After leaving office in April 1805, Burr traveled west and conspired with Louisiana Territory governor <a href="/wiki/James_Wilkinson" title="James Wilkinson">James Wilkinson</a>, beginning a large-scale recruitment for a military expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p50_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p50-205">[196]</a></sup> Other plotters included Ohio Senator <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Smith_(Ohio_Senator)" title="John Smith (Ohio Senator)">John Smith</a> and an Irishman named <a href="/wiki/Harman_Blennerhassett" title="Harman Blennerhassett">Harmon Blennerhassett</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p50_205-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p50-205">[196]</a></sup> Burr discussed a number of plots&#8212;seizing control of Mexico or Spanish Florida, or forming a secessionist state in New Orleans or the Western U.S. Historians remain unclear as to his true goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[j]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the fall of 1806, Burr launched a military flotilla carrying about 60 men down the <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a>. Wilkinson renounced the plot, apparently from self-interested motives; he reported Burr\'s expedition to Jefferson, who immediately ordered Burr\'s arrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_2002_p50_205-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_2002_p50-205">[196]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_p37_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_p37-210">[200]</a></sup> On February 13, 1807, Burr was captured in Louisiana\'s Bayou Pierre wilderness and sent to Virginia to be tried for treason.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Burr_Conspiracy_2000_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Burr_Conspiracy_2000-204">[195]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Burr\'s 1807 conspiracy trial became a national issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[201]</a></sup> Jefferson attempted to preemptively influence the verdict by telling Congress that Burr\'s guilt was "beyond question", but the case came before his longtime political foe <a href="/wiki/John_Marshall" title="John Marshall">John Marshall</a>, who dismissed the treason charge. Burr\'s legal team at one stage subpoenaed Jefferson, but Jefferson refused to testify, making the first argument for <a href="/wiki/Executive_privilege" title="Executive privilege">executive privilege</a>. Instead, Jefferson provided relevant legal documents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[202]</a></sup> After a three-month trial, the jury found Burr not guilty, while Jefferson denounced his acquittal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banner37_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banner37-213">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[204]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[k]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[205]</a></sup> Jefferson subsequently removed Wilkinson as territorial governor but retained him in the U.S. military. Historian James N. Banner criticized Jefferson for continuing to trust Wilkinson, a "faithless plotter".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banner_1974_p37_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-Banner_1974_p37-210">[200]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Chesapeake.E2.80.93Leopard_affair_and_Embargo_Act"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chesapeake&#8211;Leopard_affair_and_Embargo_Act"><i>Chesapeake</i>&#8211;<i>Leopard</i> affair and Embargo Act</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake%E2%80%93Leopard_affair" title="Chesapeake&#8211;Leopard affair">Chesapeake&#8211;Leopard affair</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leopardchesapeake.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="378" data-file-width="738" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Leopardchesapeake.jpg/300px-Leopardchesapeake.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Leopardchesapeake.jpg/450px-Leopardchesapeake.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Leopardchesapeake.jpg/600px-Leopardchesapeake.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Leopardchesapeake.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>HMS <i>Leopard</i> (right) firing upon USS <i>Chesapeake</i></div></div></div>\n<p>The British conducted raids on American shipping and kidnapped seamen in 1806&#8211;07; thousands of Americans were thus <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">impressed</a> into the British naval service. In 1806, Jefferson issued a call for a boycott of British goods; on April 18, Congress passed the Non-Importation Acts, but they were never enforced. Later that year, Jefferson asked James Monroe and <a href="/wiki/William_Pinkney" title="William Pinkney">William Pinkney</a> to negotiate with Great Britain to end the harassment of American shipping, though Britain showed no signs of improving relations. The <a href="/wiki/Monroe%E2%80%93Pinkney_Treaty" title="Monroe&#8211;Pinkney Treaty">Monroe&#8211;Pinkney Treaty</a> was finalized but lacked any provisions to end impressment, and Jefferson refused to submit it to the Senate for ratification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayes505_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hayes505-217">[206]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British ship <a href="/wiki/HMS_Leopard_(1790)" title="HMS Leopard (1790)">HMS&#160;<i>Leopard</i></a> fired upon the <a href="/wiki/USS_Chesapeake_(1799)" title="USS Chesapeake (1799)">USS&#160;<i>Chesapeake</i></a> off the Virginia coast in June 1807, and Jefferson prepared for war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-embargo_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-218">[207]</a></sup> He issued a proclamation banning armed British ships from U.S. waters. He presumed unilateral authority to call on the states to prepare 100,000 militia and ordered the purchase of arms, ammunition, and supplies, writing, "The laws of necessity, of self-preservation, of saving our country when in danger, are of higher obligation [than strict observance of written laws]". The <a href="/wiki/USS_Revenge_(1806)" title="USS Revenge (1806)">USS&#160;<i>Revenge</i></a> was dispatched to demand an explanation from the British government; it also was fired upon. Jefferson called for a special session of Congress in October to enact an embargo or alternatively to consider war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[208]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December, news arrived that Napoleon had extended the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Decree" title="Berlin Decree">Berlin Decree</a>, globally banning British imports. In Britain, <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="George III of the United Kingdom">King George III</a> ordered redoubling efforts at impressment, including American sailors. But the war fever of the summer faded; Congress had no appetite to prepare the U.S. for war. Jefferson asked for and received the Embargo Act, an alternative that allowed the U.S. more time to build up defensive works, militias, and naval forces. Later historians have seen irony in Jefferson\'s assertion of such federal power. Meacham claims that the Embargo Act was a projection of power which surpassed the Alien and Sedition Acts, and R. B. Bernstein writes that Jefferson "was pursuing policies resembling those he had cited in 1776 as grounds for independence and revolution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[209]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ograbme.jpg"><img alt="A turtle biting a man carrying a barrel to a waiting ship" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="576" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ograbme.jpg/220px-Ograbme.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ograbme.jpg/330px-Ograbme.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Ograbme.jpg/440px-Ograbme.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ograbme.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A political cartoon showing merchants dodging the "Ograbme", which is "Embargo" spelled backwards (1807)</div></div></div>\n<p>Secretary of State James Madison supported the embargo with equal vigor to Jefferson,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[210]</a></sup> while Treasury Secretary Gallatin opposed it, due to its indefinite time frame and the risk that it posed to the policy of American neutrality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[211]</a></sup> The U.S. economy suffered, criticism grew, and opponents began evading the embargo. Instead of retreating, Jefferson sent federal agents to secretly track down smugglers and violators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223"><a href="#cite_note-223">[212]</a></sup> Three acts were passed in Congress during 1807 and 1808, called the <i>Supplementary</i>, the <i>Additional</i>, and the <i>Enforcement</i> acts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-embargo_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-218">[207]</a></sup> The government could not prevent American vessels from trading with the European belligerents once they had left American ports, although the embargo triggered a devastating decline in exports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-embargo_218-2"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-218">[207]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most historians consider Jefferson\'s embargo to have been ineffective and harmful to American interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[213]</a></sup> Appleby describes the strategy as Jefferson\'s "least effective policy", and Joseph Ellis calls it "an unadulterated calamity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[214]</a></sup> Others, however, portray it as an innovative, nonviolent measure which aided France in its war with Britain while preserving American neutrality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[215]</a></sup> Jefferson believed that the failure of the embargo was due to selfish traders and merchants showing a lack of "republican virtue." He maintained that, had the embargo been widely observed, it would have avoided war in 1812.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[216]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 1807, Jefferson announced his intention not to seek a third term. He turned his attention increasingly to Monticello during the last year of his presidency, giving Madison and Gallatin almost total control of affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[217]</a></sup> Shortly before leaving office in March 1809, Jefferson signed the repeal of the Embargo. In its place, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-Intercourse_Act" title="Non-Intercourse Act">Non-Intercourse Act</a> was passed, but it proved no more effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-embargo_218-3"><a href="#cite_note-embargo-218">[207]</a></sup> The day before Madison was inaugurated as his successor, Jefferson said that he felt like "a prisoner, released from his chains".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[218]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Post-presidency_.281809.E2.80.931826.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency_(1809&#8211;1826)">Post-presidency (1809&#8211;1826)</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_and_education" title="Thomas Jefferson and education">Thomas Jefferson and education</a></div>\n<p>Following his retirement from the presidency, Jefferson continued his pursuit of educational interests; he sold his vast collection of books to the Library of Congress, and founded and built the University of Virginia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[219]</a></sup> Jefferson continued to correspond with many of the country\'s leaders, and the <a href="/wiki/Monroe_Doctrine" title="Monroe Doctrine">Monroe Doctrine</a> bears a strong resemblance to solicited advice that Jefferson gave to Monroe in 1823.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[220]</a></sup> As he settled into private life at Monticello, Jefferson developed a daily routine of rising early. He would spend several hours writing letters, with which he was often deluged. In the midday, he would often inspect the plantation on horseback. In the evenings, his family enjoyed leisure time in the gardens; late at night, Jefferson would retire to bed with a book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[221]</a></sup> However, his routine was often interrupted by uninvited visitors and tourists eager to see the icon in his final days, turning Monticello into "a virtual hotel".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[222]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_of_Virginia">University of Virginia</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Virginia" title="University of Virginia">University of Virginia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2016" data-file-width="3024" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg/300px-Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg/450px-Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg/600px-Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lawn_UVa_colorful_winter_sun_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The University of Virginia, Jefferson\'s "Academical Village"</div></div></div>\n<p>Jefferson envisioned a university free of church influences where students could specialize in many new areas not offered at other colleges. He believed that education engendered a stable society, which should provide publicly funded schools accessible to students from all social strata, based solely on ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[223]</a></sup> He initially proposed his University in a letter to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Priestley" title="Joseph Priestley">Joseph Priestley</a> in 1800<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[224]</a></sup> and, in 1819, the 76-year-old Jefferson founded the University of Virginia. He organized the state legislative campaign for its charter and, with the assistance of <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Bacon_(1785%E2%80%931866)" title="Edmund Bacon (1785&#8211;1866)">Edmund Bacon</a>, purchased the location. He was the principal designer of the buildings, planned the university\'s curriculum, and served as the first rector upon its opening in 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_1970_ch11_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_1970_ch11-236">[225]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson was a strong disciple of Greek and Roman architectural styles, which he believed to be most representative of American democracy. Each academic unit, called a pavilion, was designed with a two-story temple front, while the library "Rotunda" was modeled on the <a href="/wiki/Pantheon,_Rome" title="Pantheon, Rome">Roman Pantheon</a>. Jefferson referred to the university\'s grounds as the "<a href="/wiki/The_Lawn" title="The Lawn">Academical Village</a>," and he reflected his educational ideas in its layout. The ten pavilions included classrooms and faculty residences; they formed a quadrangle and were connected by colonnades, behind which stood the students\' rows of rooms. Gardens and vegetable plots were placed behind the pavilions and were surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Crinkle_crankle_wall" title="Crinkle crankle wall">serpentine walls</a>, affirming the importance of the agrarian lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[226]</a></sup> The university had a library rather than a church at its center, emphasizing its secular nature&#8212;a controversial aspect at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[227]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When Jefferson died in 1826, James Madison replaced him as rector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[228]</a></sup> Jefferson bequeathed most of his library to the university.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[229]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reconciliation_with_Adams">Reconciliation with Adams</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg/220px-Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg/330px-Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg/440px-Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abigail_Adams_by_Gilbert_Stuart.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1804, Abigail Adams attempted to reconcile Jefferson and Adams.</div></div></div>\n<p>Jefferson and John Adams had been good friends in the first decades of their political careers, serving together in the Continental Congress in the 1770s and in Europe in the 1780s. The Federalist/Republican split of the 1790s divided them, however, and Adams felt betrayed by Jefferson\'s sponsorship of partisan attacks, such as those of James Callender. Jefferson, on the other hand, was angered at Adams for his appointment of "midnight judges".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2008_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman2008-241">[230]</a></sup> The two men did not communicate directly for more than a decade after Jefferson succeeded Adams as president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[231]</a></sup> A brief correspondence took place between <a href="/wiki/Abigail_Adams" title="Abigail Adams">Abigail Adams</a> and Jefferson after Jefferson\'s daughter "Polly" died in 1804, in an attempt at reconciliation unknown to Adams. However, an exchange of letters resumed open hostilities between Adams and Jefferson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2008_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman2008-241">[230]</a></sup>\n</p><p>As early as 1809, <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Rush" title="Benjamin Rush">Benjamin Rush</a>, signer of the Declaration of Independence, desired that Jefferson and Adams reconcile and began to prod the two through correspondence to re-establish contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2008_241-2"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman2008-241">[230]</a></sup> In 1812, Adams wrote a short New Year\'s greeting to Jefferson, prompted earlier by Rush, to which Jefferson warmly responded. Thus began what historian <a href="/wiki/David_McCullough" title="David McCullough">David McCullough</a> calls "one of the most extraordinary correspondences in American history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[232]</a></sup> Over the next fourteen years, the former presidents exchanged 158 letters discussing their political differences, justifying their respective roles in events, and debating the revolution\'s import to the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[233]</a></sup> When Adams died, his last words included an acknowledgement of his longtime friend and rival: "Thomas Jefferson survives", unaware that Jefferson had died several hours before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[235]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Autobiography">Autobiography</span></h3>\n<p>In 1821, at the age of 77 Jefferson began writing his autobiography, in order to "state some recollections of dates and facts concerning myself".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffbio_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffbio-247">[236]</a></sup> He focused on the struggles and achievements he experienced until July 29, 1790, where the narrative stopped short.*<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[237]</a></sup> He excluded his youth, emphasizing the revolutionary era. He related that his ancestors came from Wales to America in the early 17th century and settled in the western frontier of the Virginia colony, which influenced his zeal for individual and state rights. Jefferson described his father as uneducated, but with a "strong mind and sound judgement". His enrollment in the College of William and Mary and election to the Continental Congress in Philadelphia in 1775 were included.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffbio_247-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffbio-247">[236]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He also expressed opposition to the idea of a privileged <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocracy</a> made up of large land owning families partial to the King, and instead promoted "the aristocracy of virtue and talent, which nature has wisely provided for the direction of the interests of society, &amp; scattered with equal hand through all it\'s conditions, was deemed essential to a well ordered republic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffbio_247-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffbio-247">[236]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson gave his insight about people, politics, and events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jeffbio_247-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jeffbio-247">[236]</a></sup> The work is primarily concerned with the Declaration and reforming the government of Virginia. He used notes, letters, and documents to tell many of the stories within the autobiography. He suggested that this history was so rich that his personal affairs were better overlooked, but he incorporated a self-analysis using the Declaration and other patriotism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[238]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Lafayette.27s_visit"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lafayette\'s_visit">Lafayette\'s visit</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Visit_of_the_Marquis_de_Lafayette_to_the_United_States" title="Visit of the Marquis de Lafayette to the United States">Visit of the Marquis de Lafayette to the United States</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lafayette-scheffer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1894" data-file-width="1276" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lafayette-scheffer.jpg/170px-Lafayette-scheffer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lafayette-scheffer.jpg/255px-Lafayette-scheffer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Lafayette-scheffer.jpg/340px-Lafayette-scheffer.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lafayette-scheffer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lafayette in 1824, portrait by Scheffer, hanging in U.S. House of Representatives</div></div></div>\n<p>In the summer of 1824, the <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_du_Motier,_Marquis_de_Lafayette" title="Gilbert du Motier, Marquis de Lafayette">Marquis de Lafayette</a> accepted an invitation from President James Monroe to visit the country. Jefferson and Lafayette had not seen each other since 1789. After visits to <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_England" title="New England">New England</a>, and Washington, Lafayette arrived at Monticello on November 4.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_1970_ch11_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_1970_ch11-236">[225]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson\'s grandson <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Thomas Jefferson Randolph">Randolph</a> was present and recorded the reunion: "As they approached each other, their uncertain gait quickened itself into a shuffling run, and exclaiming, \'Ah Jefferson!\' \'Ah Lafayette!\', they burst into tears as they fell into each other\'s arms." Jefferson and Lafayette then retired to the house to reminisce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[239]</a></sup> The next morning Jefferson, Lafayette, and James Madison attended a tour and banquet at the University of Virginia. Jefferson had someone else read a speech he had prepared for Lafayette, as his voice was weak and could not carry. This was his last public presentation. After an 11-day visit, Lafayette bid Jefferson goodbye and departed Monticello.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[240]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Final_days.2C_death.2C_and_burial"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_days,_death,_and_burial">Final days, death, and burial</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg"><img alt="Obelisk at Thomas Jefferson\'s gravesite" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg/170px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg/255px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg/340px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Grave_Site.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jefferson\'s gravesite</div></div></div>\n<p>Jefferson\'s approximately $100,000 of debt weighed heavily on his mind in his final months, as it became increasingly clear that he would have little to leave to his heirs. In February 1826, he successfully applied to the General Assembly to hold a public lottery as a fund raiser.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis288_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis288-252">[241]</a></sup> His health began to deteriorate in July 1825, due to a combination of rheumatism from arm and wrist injuries, as well as intestinal and urinary disorders<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peterson_1970_ch11_236-2"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_1970_ch11-236">[225]</a></sup> and, by June 1826, he was confined to bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis288_252-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis288-252">[241]</a></sup> On July 3, Jefferson was overcome by fever and declined an invitation to Washington to attend an anniversary celebration of the Declaration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[242]</a></sup>\n</p><p><span id="Death"></span>\nDuring the last hours of his life, he was accompanied by family members and friends. On July 4 at 12:50&#160;p.m., on the 50th anniversary of the Declaration of Independence, Jefferson died at age 83. His last recorded words were "No, doctor, nothing more", refusing <a href="/wiki/Laudanum" title="Laudanum">laudanum</a> from his physician, but his final significant words are often cited as "Is it the Fourth?" or "This is the Fourth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[243]</a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/John_Adams" title="John Adams">John Adams</a> died, his last words included an acknowledgement of his longtime friend and rival: "Thomas Jefferson survives", though Adams was unaware that Jefferson had died several hours before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rayner428&#8211;29_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-rayner428&#8211;29-257">[246]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berstein189_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berstein189-258">[247]</a></sup> The sitting president was Adams\'s son, <a href="/wiki/John_Quincy_Adams" title="John Quincy Adams">John Quincy Adams</a>, and he called the coincidence of their deaths on the nation\'s anniversary "visible and palpable remarks of Divine Favor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[248]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Shortly after Jefferson had died, attendants found a gold locket on a chain around his neck, where it had rested for more than 40 years, containing a small faded blue ribbon which tied a lock of his wife Martha\'s brown hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[249]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson\'s remains were buried at Monticello, under an epitaph that he wrote:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>HERE WAS BURIED THOMAS JEFFERSON, AUTHOR OF THE DECLARATION OF AMERICAN INDEPENDENCE, OF THE STATUTE OF VIRGINIA FOR RELIGIOUS FREEDOM, AND FATHER OF THE UNIVERSITY OF VIRGINIA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[250]</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>Jefferson died deeply in debt, unable to pass on his estate freely to his heirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[251]</a></sup> He gave instructions in his will for disposal of his assets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[252]</a></sup> including the freeing of Sally Hemings\'s children;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meacham,_2012,_p._495_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham,_2012,_p._495-264">[253]</a></sup> but his estate, possessions, and slaves were sold at public auctions starting in 1827.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[254]</a></sup> In 1831, Monticello was sold by <a href="/wiki/Martha_Jefferson_Randolph" title="Martha Jefferson Randolph">Martha Jefferson Randolph</a> and the other heirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[255]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Political.2C_social_and_religious_views"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Political,_social_and_religious_views">Political, social and religious views</span></h2>\n<p>Jefferson subscribed to the political ideals expounded by <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, whom he considered the three greatest men who ever lived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[257]</a></sup> He was also influenced by the writings of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Gibbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">Hume</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Robertson_(historian)" title="William Robertson (historian)">Robertson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_St_John,_1st_Viscount_Bolingbroke" title="Henry St John, 1st Viscount Bolingbroke">Bolingbroke</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[258]</a></sup> Jefferson thought that the independent <a href="/wiki/Yeoman#United_States" title="Yeoman">yeoman</a> and agrarian life were ideals of <a href="/wiki/Republicanism_in_the_United_States" title="Republicanism in the United States">republican virtues</a>. He distrusted cities and financiers, favored decentralized government power, and believed that the tyranny that had plagued the common man in Europe was due to corrupt political establishments and <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">monarchies</a>. He supported efforts to disestablish the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[259]</a></sup> wrote the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Statute_for_Religious_Freedom" title="Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom">Virginia Statute for Religious Freedom</a>, and he pressed for a <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state_in_the_United_States" title="Separation of church and state in the United States">wall of separation</a> between church and state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[260]</a></sup> The Republicans under Jefferson were strongly influenced by the 18th-century British <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whig_(British_political_party)" title="Whig (British political party)">Whig Party</a>, who believed in <a href="/wiki/Limited_government" title="Limited government">limited government</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[261]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Democratic-Republican_Party" title="Democratic-Republican Party">Democratic-Republican Party</a> became dominant in <a href="/wiki/First_Party_System" title="First Party System">early American politics</a>, and his views became known as <a href="/wiki/Jeffersonian_democracy" title="Jeffersonian democracy">Jeffersonian democracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[262]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2003_p_314_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2003_p_314-274">[263]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_government">Society and government</span></h3>\n<p>According to Jefferson\'s philosophy, citizens have "certain inalienable rights" and "rightful liberty is unobstructed action according to our will, within limits drawn around us by the equal rights of others".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[264]</a></sup> A staunch advocate of the jury system to protect people\'s liberties, he proclaimed in 1801, "I consider [trial by jury] as the only anchor yet imagined by man, by which a government can be held to the principles of its constitution."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[265]</a></sup>\nJeffersonian government not only prohibited individuals in society from infringing on the liberty of others, but also restrained itself from diminishing individual liberty as a protection against <a href="/wiki/Tyranny_of_the_majority" title="Tyranny of the majority">tyranny from the majority</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[266]</a></sup> Initially, Jefferson favored restricted voting to those who could actually have free exercise of their reason by escaping any corrupting dependence on others. He advocated enfranchising a majority of Virginians, seeking to expand suffrage to include "yeoman farmers" who owned their own land while excluding tenant farmers, city day laborers, vagrants, most Amerindians, and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood220_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood220-278">[267]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He was convinced that individual liberties were the fruit of political equality, which were threatened by arbitrary government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[268]</a></sup> Excesses of democracy in his view were caused by institutional corruptions rather than human nature. He was less suspicious of a working democracy than many contemporaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood220_278-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wood220-278">[267]</a></sup> As president, Jefferson feared that the <a href="/wiki/Federalism_in_the_United_States" title="Federalism in the United States">Federalist system</a> enacted by Washington and Adams had encouraged corrupting patronage and dependence. He tried to restore a balance between the state and federal governments more nearly reflecting the <a href="/wiki/Articles_of_Confederation" title="Articles of Confederation">Articles of Confederation</a>, seeking to reinforce state prerogatives where his party was in a majority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood220_278-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wood220-278">[267]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Jefferson was steeped in the <a href="/wiki/Whigs_(British_political_party)" title="Whigs (British political party)">British Whig</a> tradition of the oppressed majority set against a repeatedly unresponsive court party in the Parliament. He justified small outbreaks of rebellion as necessary to get monarchial regimes to amend oppressive measures compromising popular liberties.
